lang="en">Europe</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div For the politico-economic union, see <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Europe_(disambiguation)" title="Europe (disambiguation)">Europe (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Continent in the Northern Hemisphere and mostly in the Eastern Hemisphere</div>\n<p href="#ref_footnote_b">^</a></b></span>  Istanbul is a transcontinental city with about two-thirds of the population residing in the European side.</li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Europe</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continent</a> located entirely in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> and mostly in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">Eastern Hemisphere</a>. It is bordered by the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a> to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the west and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> to the south. It comprises the westernmost part of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a>.\n</p><p>Since around 1850, Europe is most commonly considered to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_continents#Europe_and_Asia" title="Borders of the continents">separated from Asia</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">watershed divides</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">Caucasus Mountains</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ural_River" title="Ural River">Ural River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black</a> Seas and the waterways of the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Straits" title="Turkish Straits">Turkish Straits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatlGeoAtlas_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatlGeoAtlas-7">[7]</a></sup> Although the term "continent" implies <a href="/wiki/Physical_geography" title="Physical geography">physical geography</a>, the land border is somewhat arbitrary and has been redefined several times since its first conception in <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>. The division of Eurasia into two continents reflects <a href="/wiki/East%E2%80%93West_dichotomy" title="East&#8211;West dichotomy">East-West</a> cultural, linguistic and ethnic differences which vary on a spectrum rather than with a sharp dividing line. The geographic border also does not follow political boundaries, with <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> being <a href="/wiki/List_of_transcontinental_countries" title="List of transcontinental countries">transcontinental countries</a>.  A strict application of the Caucasus Mountains boundary also places two comparatively small countries, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, in both continents.\n</p><p>Europe covers about 10,180,000 square kilometres (3,930,000&#160;sq&#160;mi), or 2% of the Earth\'s surface (6.8% of land area). Politically, Europe is divided into about <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_in_Europe" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories in Europe">fifty sovereign states</a> of which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">Russian Federation</a> is the largest and most populous, spanning 39% of the continent and comprising 15% of its population. Europe had a <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Europe" title="Demographics of Europe">total population</a> of about 741 million (about 11% of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a>) as of  2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Europe&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_climate" title="European climate">European climate</a> is largely affected by warm Atlantic currents that temper winters and summers on much of the continent, even at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latitudes" title="Latitudes">latitudes</a> along which the climate in Asia and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> is severe. Further from the sea, seasonal differences are more noticeable than close to the coast.\n</p><p>Europe, in particular <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>, was the birthplace of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">Western civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duchesne2011_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duchesne2011-10">[10]</a></sup> The fall of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> in 476 AD and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a> marked the end of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient history</a> and the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">Renaissance humanism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_exploration" title="Renaissance exploration">exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_art" title="Renaissance art">art</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_science" title="Renaissance science">science</a> led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_era" title="Modern era">modern era</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a> started by <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, Europe played a predominant role in global affairs. Between the 16th and 20th centuries, European powers <a href="/wiki/European_colonialism" title="European colonialism">controlled</a> at various times the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, almost all of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> and the majority of Asia.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, the subsequent <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> shaped the continent culturally, politically and economically from the end of the 17th century until the first half of the 19th century. The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, which began in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> at the end of the 18th century, gave rise to radical economic, cultural and social change in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> and eventually the wider world. Both <a href="/wiki/World_war" title="World war">world wars</a> took place for the most part in Europe, contributing to a decline in Western European dominance in world affairs by the mid-20th century as the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> took prominence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_534_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_534-11">[11]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, Europe was divided along the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain" title="Iron Curtain">Iron Curtain</a> between <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> in the West and the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> in the East, until the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1989" title="Revolutions of 1989">revolutions of 1989</a> and <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall#Fall_of_the_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">fall of the Berlin Wall</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_subregion_map_world_factbook.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="680" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Europe_subregion_map_world_factbook.svg/300px-Europe_subregion_map_world_factbook.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Europe_subregion_map_world_factbook.svg/450px-Europe_subregion_map_world_factbook.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Europe_subregion_map_world_factbook.svg/600px-Europe_subregion_map_world_factbook.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_subregion_map_world_factbook.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Subregions of Europe - CIA World Factbook <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #007FFF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Northern Europe</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00FFFF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Western Europe</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F0DC82; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Central Europe</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #66FF00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Southern Europe</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #D2691E; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Southeastern Europe</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Southwestern Europe</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #F88379; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Eastern Europe</div></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1949 the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> was founded, following a speech by Sir <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, with the idea of unifying Europe to achieve common goals. It includes all European states except for <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a>. Further <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a> by some states led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU), a separate political entity that lies between a <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederation</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The EU originated in Western Europe but has been <a href="/wiki/Enlargement_of_the_European_Union" title="Enlargement of the European Union">expanding eastward</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">fall of the Soviet Union</a> in 1991. The currency of most countries of the European Union, the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a>, is the most commonly used among Europeans; and the EU\'s <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">Schengen Area</a> abolishes border and immigration controls among most of its member states. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Anthem" title="European Anthem">European Anthem</a> is "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ode_to_Joy_(Beethoven)" title="Ode to Joy (Beethoven)">Ode to Joy</a>", and states celebrate peace and unity on <a href="/wiki/Europe_Day" title="Europe Day">Europe Day</a>. \n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872693506">.mw-parser-output .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>\n<p><span id="Etymology"></span>\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_(mythology)" title="Europa (mythology)">Europa (mythology)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_3.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1936" decoding="async" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_3.jpeg/220px-Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_3.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_3.jpeg/330px-Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_3.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_3.jpeg/440px-Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_3.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_3.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue representing Europa at <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Ferreria" title="Palazzo Ferreria">Palazzo Ferreria</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Valletta" title="Valletta">Valletta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_World_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Herodotus_World_Map.jpg/220px-Herodotus_World_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Herodotus_World_Map.jpg/330px-Herodotus_World_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Herodotus_World_Map.jpg/440px-Herodotus_World_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_World_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>\' world map (450 BC)</div></div></div>\n<p>In classical <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_(mythology)" title="Europa (mythology)">Europa</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">&#917;&#8016;&#961;&#974;&#960;&#951;</span>, <i>Eur&#7763;p&#275;</i>) was a <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> princess. The word Europe is derived from her name. The name contains the elements &#949;&#8016;&#961;&#973;&#962; (<i>eur&#250;s</i>), "wide, broad"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and &#8036;&#968; (<i>&#333;ps</i>, gen. &#8032;&#960;&#972;&#962;, <i>&#333;p&#243;s</i>) "eye, face, countenance",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> hence their composite <i>Eur&#7763;p&#275;</i> would mean "wide-gazing" or "broad of aspect". <i>Broad</i> has been an <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithet</a> of Earth herself in the reconstructed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion" title="Proto-Indo-European religion">Proto-Indo-European religion</a> and the poetry devoted to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There have been attempts to connect <i>Eur&#7763;p&#275;</i> to a Semitic term for "west", this being either <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian</a> <i>erebu</i> meaning "to go down, set" (said of the sun) or <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician</a> <i> \'ereb</i> "evening, west",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> which is at the origin of Arabic <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> and Hebrew <i>ma\'arav</i>. <a href="/wiki/Michael_A._Barry" title="Michael A. Barry">Michael A. Barry</a>, professor in <a href="/wiki/Princeton_University" title="Princeton University">Princeton University</a>\'s Near Eastern Studies Department, finds the mention of the word <i>Ereb</i> on an Assyrian stele with the meaning of "night, [the country of] sunset", in opposition to <i>Asu</i> "[the country of] sunrise", i.e. Asia. The same naming motive according to "cartographic convention" appears in Greek &#7944;&#957;&#945;&#964;&#959;&#955;&#942; (<i>Anatol&#7703;</i> "[sun] rise", "east", hence <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Litchfield_West" title="Martin Litchfield West">Martin Litchfield West</a> stated that "phonologically, the match between Europa\'s name and any form of the Semitic word is very poor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Next to these hypotheses there is also a <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> root <i>*h<sub>1</sub>reg&#695;os</i>, meaning "darkness", which also produced Greek <i><a href="/wiki/Erebus" title="Erebus">Erebus</a></i>.\n</p><p>Most major world languages use words derived from <i>Eur&#7763;p&#275;</i> or <i>Europa</i> to refer to the continent. Chinese, for example, uses the word <i>&#332;uzh&#333;u</i> (&#27472;&#27954;/&#27431;&#27954;); a similar Chinese-derived term <i>&#332;sh&#363;</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja">&#27431;&#24030;</span>)</span> is also sometimes used in Japanese such as in the Japanese name of the European Union, <i>&#332;sh&#363; Reng&#333;</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja">&#27431;&#24030;&#36899;&#21512;</span>)</span>, despite the <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> <i>Y&#333;roppa</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja">&#12520;&#12540;&#12525;&#12483;&#12497;</span>)</span> being more commonly used. In some Turkic languages the originally Persian name <i><a href="/wiki/Frangistan" title="Frangistan">Frangistan</a></i> ("land of the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>") is used casually in referring to much of Europe, besides official names such as <i>Avrupa</i> or <i>Evropa</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davison_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-davison-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_transcontinental_countries" title="List of transcontinental countries">List of transcontinental countries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boundaries_between_the_continents_of_Earth" title="Boundaries between the continents of Earth">Boundaries between the continents of Earth</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_definition">Contemporary definition</span></h3>\n<center>\n<div class="thumbinner overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">\n<p><small>Clickable map of Europe, showing one of the most commonly used <a href="/wiki/Boundaries_between_the_continents_of_Earth" title="Boundaries between the continents of Earth">continental boundaries</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <br/><b>Key:</b> <span style="color:blue"><b>blue</b></span>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcontinental_country" title="Transcontinental country">states which straddle the border between Europe and Asia</a>;\n<span style="color:green"><b>green</b></span>: countries not geographically in Europe, but closely associated with the continent\n</small>\n</p>\n</div>\n<div class="noresize" role="img" style="width: 700px; left:526px; top:401.5px"><a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea"><span style="color:#E2EBF5"><i>Black<br/>Sea</i></span></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:645px; top:344.1px"><a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea"><span style="color:#E2EBF5"><i>Caspian<br/>Sea</i></span></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:211px; top:317.5px"><a href="/wiki/Celtic_Sea" title="Celtic Sea"><span style="color:#E2EBF5"><i>Celtic<br/>Sea</i></span></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:162px; top:118px"><i><a href="/wiki/Greenland_Sea" title="Greenland Sea"><span style="color:#E2EBF5;">Greenland Sea</span></a></i></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:78px; top:41px"><i><a href="/wiki/Baffin_Bay" title="Baffin Bay"><span style="color:#E2EBF5;">Baffin Bay</span></a></i></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:145.9px; top:468px"><a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_C%C3%A1diz" title="Gulf of C&#225;diz"><span style="color:#E2EBF5"><i>Gulf of<br/>C&#225;diz</i></span></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:288px; top:433px"><a href="/wiki/Ligurian_Sea" title="Ligurian Sea"><span style="color:#E2EBF5"><i>Ligurian<br/>Sea</i></span></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:288px; top:485.5px"><i><a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea"><span style="color:#E2EBF5;">Mediterranean Sea</span></a></i></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:120px; top:223px"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Ocean" title="North Atlantic Ocean"><span style="color:#E2EBF5"><i>North<br/>Atlantic<br/>Ocean</i></span></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:302px; top:279px"><a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea"><span style="color:#E2EBF5"><i>North<br/>Sea</i></span></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:295px; top:181px"><a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea"><span style="color:#E2EBF5"><i>Norwegian<br/>Sea</i></span></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:186.5px; top:486.2px"><i><a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar"><span style="color:#E2EBF5;">Strait of Gibraltar</span></a></i></div>\n</div>\n</center>\n<p>The prevalent definition of Europe as a geographical term has been in use since the mid-19th century.\nEurope is taken to be bounded by large bodies of water to the north, west and south; Europe\'s limits to the far east are usually taken to be the Urals, the <a href="/wiki/Ural_River" title="Ural River">Ural River</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>; to the southeast, including the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus_Mountains" title="Caucasus Mountains">Caucasus Mountains</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and the waterways connecting the Black Sea to the Mediterranean Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encarta_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Islands are generally grouped with the nearest continental landmass, hence <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> is generally considered to be part of Europe, while the nearby island of <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> is usually assigned to <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. Nevertheless, there are some exceptions based on sociopolitical and cultural differences. <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> is closest to <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia (or Asia Minor)</a>, but is usually considered part of Europe both culturally and politically and is a member state of the EU. <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> was considered an island of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northwest_Africa" title="Northwest Africa">Northwest Africa</a> for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>"Europe" as used specifically in <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a> may also refer to <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a> exclusively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_concept">History of the concept</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="864" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/220px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/330px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg/440px-T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T_and_O_map_Guntherus_Ziner_1472.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A medieval <a href="/wiki/T_and_O_map" title="T and O map">T and O map</a> from 1472 showing the three continents as domains of the sons of <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a> &#8212; Asia to Sem (<a href="/wiki/Shem" title="Shem">Shem</a>), Europe to Iafeth (<a href="/wiki/Japheth" title="Japheth">Japheth</a>), and Africa to Cham (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ham,_son_of_Noah" title="Ham, son of Noah">Ham</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>The first recorded usage of <i>Eur&#7763;p&#275;</i> as a geographic term is in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric_Hymn" title="Homeric Hymn">Homeric Hymn</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delian_Apollo" title="Delian Apollo">Delian Apollo</a>, in reference to the western shore of the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>. As a name for a part of the known world, it is first used in the 6th century BC by <a href="/wiki/Anaximander" title="Anaximander">Anaximander</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">Hecataeus</a>. Anaximander placed the boundary between Asia and Europe along the Phasis River (the modern <a href="/wiki/Rioni_River" title="Rioni River">Rioni River</a>) in the Caucasus, a convention still followed by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> in the 5th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Herodotus mentioned that the world had been divided by unknown persons into three parts, Europe, Asia, and Libya (Africa), with the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> and the Phasis forming their boundaries&#8212;though he also states that some considered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" title="Don River (Russia)">River Don</a>, rather than the Phasis, as the boundary between Europe and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Europe\'s eastern frontier was defined in the 1st century by geographer <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> at the River Don.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jubilees" title="Jubilees">Book of Jubilees</a></i> described the continents as the lands given by <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a> to his three sons; Europe was defined as stretching from the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Hercules" title="Pillars of Hercules">Pillars of Hercules</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a>, separating it from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northwest_Africa" title="Northwest Africa">Northwest Africa</a>, to the Don, separating it from Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The convention received by the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and surviving into modern usage is that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_era" title="Roman era">Roman era</a> used by Roman era authors such as <a href="/wiki/Posidonius" title="Posidonius">Posidonius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\nwho took the Tanais (the modern Don River) as the boundary.\n</p><p>The term "Europe" is first used for a cultural sphere in the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a> of the 9th century. From that time, the term designated the sphere of influence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Church" title="Western Church">Western Church</a>, as opposed to both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> churches and to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>.\n</p><p>A cultural definition of Europe as the lands of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Latin Christendom</a> coalesced in the 8th century, signifying the new cultural condominium created through the confluence of Germanic traditions and Christian-Latin culture, defined partly in contrast with <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and limited to northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberia" title="Iberia">Iberia</a>, the British Isles, France, Christianised western Germany, the Alpine regions and northern and central Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The concept is one of the lasting legacies of the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a>: <i>Europa</i> often<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2016)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Europe#Dubious" title="Talk:Europe">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> figures in the letters of Charlemagne\'s court scholar, <a href="/wiki/Alcuin" title="Alcuin">Alcuin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_definitions">Modern definitions</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Europe" title="Regions of Europe">Regions of Europe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_Europe" title="Core Europe">Core Europe</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europa_Prima_Pars_Terrae_in_Forma_Virginis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="1799" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Europa_Prima_Pars_Terrae_in_Forma_Virginis.jpg/270px-Europa_Prima_Pars_Terrae_in_Forma_Virginis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Europa_Prima_Pars_Terrae_in_Forma_Virginis.jpg/405px-Europa_Prima_Pars_Terrae_in_Forma_Virginis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Europa_Prima_Pars_Terrae_in_Forma_Virginis.jpg/540px-Europa_Prima_Pars_Terrae_in_Forma_Virginis.jpg 2x" width="270"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europa_Prima_Pars_Terrae_in_Forma_Virginis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of <i><a href="/wiki/Europa_regina" title="Europa regina">Europa regina</a></i> (\'Queen Europe\') in 1582.</div></div></div>\n<p>The question of defining a precise eastern boundary of Europe arises in the Early Modern period, as the eastern extension of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscovy" title="Muscovy">Muscovy</a> began to include Northern Asia.\n</p><p>Throughout the Middle Ages and into the 18th century, the traditional division of the landmass of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> into two continents, Europe and Asia, followed Ptolemy, with the boundary following the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Straits" title="Turkish Straits">Turkish Straits</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kerch_Strait" title="Kerch Strait">Kerch Strait</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Azov" title="Sea of Azov">Sea of Azov</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" title="Don River (Russia)">Don</a> (ancient <a href="/wiki/Tanais" title="Tanais">Tanais</a>). But maps produced during the 16th to 18th centuries tended to differ in how to continue the boundary beyond the Don bend at <a href="/wiki/Kalach-na-Donu" title="Kalach-na-Donu">Kalach-na-Donu</a> (where it is closest to the Volga, now joined with it by the <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Don_Canal" title="Volga&#8211;Don Canal">Volga&#8211;Don Canal</a>), into territory not described in any detail by the ancient geographers.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philip_Johan_von_Strahlenberg" title="Philip Johan von Strahlenberg">Philip Johan von Strahlenberg</a> in 1725 was the first to depart from the classical Don boundary by proposing that mountain ranges could be included as boundaries between continents whenever there were no suitable waterways.  He drew a new line along the <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" title="Volga River">Volga</a>, following the Volga north until the <a href="/wiki/Samara_Bend" title="Samara Bend">Samara Bend</a>, along <a href="/wiki/Obshchy_Syrt" title="Obshchy Syrt">Obshchy Syrt</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">drainage divide</a> between Volga and <a href="/wiki/Ural_River" title="Ural River">Ural</a>) and then north along <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This was adopted by the Russian Empire, and introduced the convention that would eventually become commonly accepted, but not without criticism by many modern analytical geographers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The mapmakers continued to differ on the boundary between the lower Don and Samara well into the 19th century. The <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Atlas_of_Russian_Empire._1745_year" title="commons:Category:Atlas of Russian Empire. 1745 year">1745 atlas</a> published by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">Russian Academy of Sciences</a> has the boundary follow the Don beyond Kalach as far as <a href="/wiki/Serafimovich_(town)" title="Serafimovich (town)">Serafimovich</a> before cutting north towards <a href="/wiki/Arkhangelsk" title="Arkhangelsk">Arkhangelsk</a>, while other 18th- to 19th-century mapmakers such as <a href="/wiki/John_Cary" title="John Cary">John Cary</a> followed Strahlenberg\'s prescription. To the south, the <a href="/wiki/Kuma%E2%80%93Manych_Depression" title="Kuma&#8211;Manych Depression">Kuma&#8211;Manych Depression</a> was identified circa 1773 by a German naturalist, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Simon_Pallas" title="Peter Simon Pallas">Peter Simon Pallas</a>, as a valley that, once upon a time, connected the Black Sea and the Caspian Sea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oren-icn.ru_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-oren-icn.ru-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and subsequently was proposed as a natural boundary between continents.\n</p><p>By the mid-19th century, there were three main conventions, one following the Don, the <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Don_Canal" title="Volga&#8211;Don Canal">Volga&#8211;Don Canal</a> and the Volga, the other following the Kuma&#8211;Manych Depression to the Caspian and then the Ural River, and the third abandoning the Don altogether, following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Caucasus_watershed" title="Greater Caucasus watershed">Greater Caucasus watershed</a> to the Caspian. The question was still treated as a "controversy" in geographical literature of the 1860s, with <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Freshfield" title="Douglas Freshfield">Douglas Freshfield</a> advocating the Caucasus crest boundary as the "best possible", citing support from various "modern geographers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, the boundary along the Kuma&#8211;Manych Depression was the most commonly used as early as 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1958, the Soviet Geographical Society formally recommended that the boundary between the Europe and Asia be drawn in textbooks from <a href="/wiki/Baydaratskaya_Bay" title="Baydaratskaya Bay">Baydaratskaya Bay</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Kara_Sea" title="Kara Sea">Kara Sea</a>, along the eastern foot of Ural Mountains, then following the <a href="/wiki/Ural_River" title="Ural River">Ural River</a> until the <a href="/wiki/Mugodzhar_Hills" title="Mugodzhar Hills">Mugodzhar Hills</a>, and then the <a href="/wiki/Emba_River" title="Emba River">Emba River</a>; and Kuma&#8211;Manych Depression,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> thus placing the Caucasus entirely in Asia and the Urals entirely in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> However, most geographers in the Soviet Union favoured the boundary along the Caucasus crest<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and this became the common convention in the later 20th century, although the Kuma&#8211;Manych boundary remained in use in some 20th-century maps.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe" title="History of Europe">History of Europe</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Europe" title="Prehistoric Europe">Prehistoric Europe</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lascaux_painting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Lascaux_painting.jpg/260px-Lascaux_painting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Lascaux_painting.jpg/390px-Lascaux_painting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Lascaux_painting.jpg/520px-Lascaux_painting.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lascaux_painting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paleolithic cave paintings from <a href="/wiki/Lascaux" title="Lascaux">Lascaux</a> in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> (c 15,000 BC)</div></div></div>\n<p><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_erectus_georgicus" title="Homo erectus georgicus">Homo erectus georgicus</a></i>, which lived roughly 1.8&#160;million years ago in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, is the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominid</a> to have been discovered in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Other hominid remains, dating back roughly 1&#160;million years, have been discovered in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological_Site_of_Atapuerca" title="Archaeological Site of Atapuerca">Atapuerca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthal_man" title="Neanderthal man">Neanderthal man</a> (named after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neandertal" title="Neandertal">Neandertal</a> valley in <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>) appeared in Europe 150,000 years ago (115,000 years ago it is found already in Poland<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>) and disappeared from the fossil record about 28,000 years ago, with their final refuge being present-day <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>. The Neanderthals were supplanted by modern humans (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cro-Magnons" title="Cro-Magnons">Cro-Magnons</a>), who appeared in Europe around 43,000 to 40,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_21_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_21-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stonehenge,_Condado_de_Wiltshire,_Inglaterra,_2014-08-12,_DD_09.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2379" data-file-width="5406" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Stonehenge%2C_Condado_de_Wiltshire%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-12%2C_DD_09.JPG/220px-Stonehenge%2C_Condado_de_Wiltshire%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-12%2C_DD_09.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Stonehenge%2C_Condado_de_Wiltshire%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-12%2C_DD_09.JPG/330px-Stonehenge%2C_Condado_de_Wiltshire%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-12%2C_DD_09.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Stonehenge%2C_Condado_de_Wiltshire%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-12%2C_DD_09.JPG/440px-Stonehenge%2C_Condado_de_Wiltshire%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-12%2C_DD_09.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stonehenge,_Condado_de_Wiltshire,_Inglaterra,_2014-08-12,_DD_09.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (Late Neolithic from 3000&#8211;2000 BC).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vinca_clay_figure_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2160" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Vinca_clay_figure_02.jpg/170px-Vinca_clay_figure_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Vinca_clay_figure_02.jpg/255px-Vinca_clay_figure_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Vinca_clay_figure_02.jpg/340px-Vinca_clay_figure_02.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vinca_clay_figure_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neolithic terracotta figurine from <a href="/wiki/Vin%C4%8Da_culture" title="Vin&#269;a culture">Vin&#269;a</a> in <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> (4500&#8211;4000 BC)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nebra_Scheibe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1584" data-file-width="1611" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Nebra_Scheibe.jpg/220px-Nebra_Scheibe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Nebra_Scheibe.jpg/330px-Nebra_Scheibe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Nebra_Scheibe.jpg/440px-Nebra_Scheibe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nebra_Scheibe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nebra_sky_disk" title="Nebra sky disk">Nebra sky disk</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> (1600 BC), <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Neolithic" title="European Neolithic">European Neolithic</a> period&#8212;marked by the cultivation of crops and the raising of livestock, increased numbers of settlements and the widespread use of pottery&#8212;began around 7000 BC in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, probably influenced by earlier farming practices in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borza_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borza-46">[46]</a></sup> It spread from the Balkans along the valleys of the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Linear_Pottery_culture" title="Linear Pottery culture">Linear Pottery culture</a>) and along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_coast" title="Mediterranean coast">Mediterranean coast</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardial_Ware" title="Cardial Ware">Cardial culture</a>). Between 4500 and 3000 BC, these central European neolithic cultures developed further to the west and the north, transmitting newly acquired skills in producing copper artefacts. In Western Europe the Neolithic period was characterised not by large agricultural settlements but by field monuments, such as <a href="/wiki/Causewayed_enclosure" title="Causewayed enclosure">causewayed enclosures</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burial_mound" title="Burial mound">burial mounds</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalithic_tomb" title="Megalithic tomb">megalithic tombs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corded_Ware" title="Corded Ware">Corded Ware</a> cultural horizon flourished at the transition from the Neolithic to the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a>. During this period giant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalithic" title="Megalithic">megalithic</a> monuments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Megalithic_Temples_of_Malta" title="Megalithic Temples of Malta">Megalithic Temples of Malta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>, were constructed throughout Western and Southern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Bronze_Age" title="European Bronze Age">European Bronze Age</a> began c. 3200 BC in Greece with the <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan civilization</a> on <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, the first advanced civilization in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The Minoans were followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenean_Greece" title="Mycenean Greece">Myceneans</a>, who collapsed suddenly around 1200 BC, ushering the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Iron_Age" title="European Iron Age">European Iron Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Iron Age colonisation by the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenicians" title="Phoenicians">Phoenicians</a> gave rise to early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_basin" title="Mediterranean basin">Mediterranean</a> cities. Early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Age_Italy" title="Iron Age Italy">Iron Age Italy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaic_Greece" title="Archaic Greece">Greece</a> from around the 8th century BC gradually gave rise to historical Classical antiquity, whose beginning is sometimes dated to 776 BC, the year the first <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity">Classical antiquity</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="804" data-file-width="1144" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg/220px-The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg/330px-The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg/440px-The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> (432 BC)</div></div></div>\n<p>Ancient Greece was the founding culture of Western civilisation. Western <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalist culture</a> are often attributed to Ancient Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daly2013_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daly2013-53">[53]</a></sup> The Greeks city-state, the <a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">polis</a>, was the fundamental political unit of classical Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daly2013_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Daly2013-53">[53]</a></sup> In 508 BC, <a href="/wiki/Cleisthenes" title="Cleisthenes">Cleisthenes</a> instituted the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">democratic</a> system of government in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BKDunn1992_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-BKDunn1992-54">[54]</a></sup> The Greek political ideals were rediscovered in the late 18th century by European philosophers and idealists. Greece also generated many cultural contributions: in <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> under <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">history</a> with <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thucydides" title="Thucydides">Thucydides</a>; in dramatic and narrative verse, starting with the epic poems of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_76_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_76-55">[55]</a></sup> in drama with <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a>, in medicine with <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>; and in science with <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heath_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heath-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heath_Vol_2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heath_Vol_2-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In the course of the 5th century BC, several of the Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_states" title="City states">city states</a> would ultimately check the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Persian</a> advance in Europe through the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a>, considered a pivotal moment in world history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strauss2005_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strauss2005-59">[59]</a></sup> as the 50 years of peace that followed are known as <a href="/wiki/Fifth-century_Athens" title="Fifth-century Athens">Golden Age of Athens</a>, the seminal period of ancient Greece that laid many of the foundations of Western civilization.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomanEmpire_117.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2180" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/RomanEmpire_117.svg/260px-RomanEmpire_117.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/RomanEmpire_117.svg/390px-RomanEmpire_117.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/RomanEmpire_117.svg/520px-RomanEmpire_117.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomanEmpire_117.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> at its greatest extent in 117 AD.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Bevilacqua_Glyptothek_Munich_317.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2209" data-file-width="1529" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Augustus_Bevilacqua_Glyptothek_Munich_317.jpg/170px-Augustus_Bevilacqua_Glyptothek_Munich_317.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Augustus_Bevilacqua_Glyptothek_Munich_317.jpg/255px-Augustus_Bevilacqua_Glyptothek_Munich_317.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Augustus_Bevilacqua_Glyptothek_Munich_317.jpg/340px-Augustus_Bevilacqua_Glyptothek_Munich_317.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augustus_Bevilacqua_Glyptothek_Munich_317.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Augustus wearing the <a href="/wiki/Civic_Crown" title="Civic Crown">Civic Crown</a> at Palazzo Bevilacqua in <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Greece was followed by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a>, which left its mark on <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">politics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_engineering" title="Roman engineering">engineering</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_architecture" title="Roman architecture">architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centralized_government" title="Centralized government">government</a> and many more key aspects in western civilisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daly2013_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Daly2013-53">[53]</a></sup> Expanding from their base in Italy beginning in the 3rd century BC, the Romans gradually expanded to eventually rule the entire Mediterranean basin and western Europe by the turn of the millennium. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> ended in 27 BC, when <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. The two centuries that followed are known as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pax_romana" title="Pax romana">pax romana</a></i>, a period of unprecedented peace, prosperity, and political stability in most of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mieawl_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-mieawl-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The empire continued to expand under emperors such as <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>, who spent time on the Empire\'s northern border fighting <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_123_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_123-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The Empire began to decline in the 3rd century, particularly in the west. <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great_and_Christianity" title="Constantine the Great and Christianity">legalised</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> in 313 AD after three centuries of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persecution_of_early_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of early Christians in the Roman Empire">imperial persecution</a>. Constantine also permanently moved the capital of the empire from Rome to the city of <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, which was renamed <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> in his honour (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>) in 330 AD. Christianity became the sole official religion of the empire in 380 AD, and in 391-392 AD, the emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius</a> outlawed pagan religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FriellWilliams2005_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FriellWilliams2005-63">[63]</a></sup> This is sometimes considered to mark the end of antiquity; alternatively antiquity is considered to end with the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a> in 476 AD; the closure of the pagan <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Platonic Academy of Athens</a> in 529 AD;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> or the rise of Islam in the early 7th century AD.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages (historiography)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Migrations" title="Age of Migrations">Age of Migrations</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:244px;max-width:244px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:242px;max-width:242px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_around_650.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2392" data-file-width="3891" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Europe_around_650.jpg/240px-Europe_around_650.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Europe_around_650.jpg/360px-Europe_around_650.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Europe_around_650.jpg/480px-Europe_around_650.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Europe c. 650</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:242px;max-width:242px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1205" data-file-width="1591" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg/240px-Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg/360px-Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg/480px-Frankish_Empire_481_to_814-en.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Charlemagne\'s empire</a> in 814: <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:#3CB371; color:#3CB371;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Francia</span>, <span style="margin:0px; font-size:90%;"><span style="border:none; background-color:#FAEBD7; color:#FAEBD7;">&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;</span>&#160;Tributaries</span></div></div></div>\n<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">decline of the Roman Empire</a>, Europe entered a long period of change arising from what historians call the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Migrations" title="Age of Migrations">Age of Migrations</a>". There were numerous invasions and migrations amongst the <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pannonian_Avars" title="Pannonian Avars">Avars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgars" title="Bulgars">Bulgars</a> and, later on, the <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pechenegs" title="Pechenegs">Pechenegs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumans" title="Cumans">Cumans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magyars" title="Magyars">Magyars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mieawl_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-mieawl-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> would later refer to this as the "Dark Ages".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Isolated monastic communities were the only places to safeguard and compile written knowledge accumulated previously; apart from this very few written records survive and much literature, philosophy, mathematics, and other thinking from the classical period disappeared from Western Europe though they were preserved in the east, in the Byzantine Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the Roman empire in the west continued to decline, Roman traditions and the Roman state remained strong in the predominantly Greek-speaking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. During most of its existence, the Byzantine Empire was the most powerful economic, cultural, and military force in Europe. Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> presided over Constantinople\'s first golden age: he established a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_of_Justinian" title="Code of Justinian">legal code</a> that forms the basis of many modern legal systems, funded the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, and brought the Christian church under state control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_135_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_135-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:164px;max-width:164px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rolandfealty.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="507" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Rolandfealty.jpg/160px-Rolandfealty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Rolandfealty.jpg/240px-Rolandfealty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Rolandfealty.jpg/320px-Rolandfealty.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Roland" title="Roland">Roland</a> pledges <a href="/wiki/Fealty" title="Fealty">fealty</a> to <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delegation_of_Croats_and_Serbs_to_Emperor_Basil_I,_Skylitzes.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="1081" decoding="async" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Delegation_of_Croats_and_Serbs_to_Emperor_Basil_I%2C_Skylitzes.jpg/160px-Delegation_of_Croats_and_Serbs_to_Emperor_Basil_I%2C_Skylitzes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Delegation_of_Croats_and_Serbs_to_Emperor_Basil_I%2C_Skylitzes.jpg/240px-Delegation_of_Croats_and_Serbs_to_Emperor_Basil_I%2C_Skylitzes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Delegation_of_Croats_and_Serbs_to_Emperor_Basil_I%2C_Skylitzes.jpg/320px-Delegation_of_Croats_and_Serbs_to_Emperor_Basil_I%2C_Skylitzes.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Delegations of <a href="/wiki/Croats" title="Croats">Croats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> at Byzantine court of <a href="/wiki/Basil_I" title="Basil I">Basil I</a></div></div></div>\n<p>From the 7th century onwards, as the Byzantines and neighbouring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sasanids" title="Sasanids">Sasanid Persians</a> were severely weakened due the protracted, centuries-lasting and frequent <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_wars" title="Byzantine&#8211;Sasanian wars">Byzantine&#8211;Sasanian wars</a>, the Muslim Arabs began to make inroads into historically Roman territory, taking the Levant and North Africa and making inroads into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a>. In the mid 7th century AD, following the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Persia</a>, Islam penetrated into the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Over the next centuries Muslim forces took <a href="/wiki/Cyprus_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Cyprus in the Middle Ages">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Crete" title="Emirate of Crete">Crete</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sicily" title="Emirate of Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam_in_southern_Italy" title="History of Islam in southern Italy">parts of southern Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Between 711 and 720, most of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> was brought under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> rule&#160;&#8212; save for small areas in the northwest (<a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">Asturias</a>) and largely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basque_people" title="Basque people">Basque</a> regions in the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>. This territory, under the Arabic name <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>, became part of the expanding <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>. The unsuccessful <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Constantinople_(717%E2%80%93718)" title="Siege of Constantinople (717&#8211;718)">second siege of Constantinople</a> (717) weakened the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">Umayyad dynasty</a> and reduced their prestige. The Umayyads were then defeated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frankish_Empire" title="Frankish Empire">Frankish</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tours" title="Battle of Tours">Battle of Poitiers</a> in 732, which ended their northward advance.\n</p><p>During the Dark Ages, the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> fell under the control of various tribes. The Germanic and Slav tribes established their domains over Western and Eastern Europe respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_143_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_143-70">[70]</a></sup> Eventually the Frankish tribes were united under <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis&#160;I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_162_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_162-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, a Frankish king of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian" title="Carolingian">Carolingian</a> dynasty who had conquered most of Western Europe, was anointed "<a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>" by the Pope in 800. This led in 962 to the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>, which eventually became centred in the German principalities of central Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_166_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo_166-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Central_Europe" title="East Central Europe">East Central Europe</a> saw the creation of the first Slavic states and the adoption of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> (circa 1000 AD). The powerful <a href="/wiki/West_Slavs" title="West Slavs">West Slavic</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Great_Moravia" title="Great Moravia">Great Moravia</a> spread its territory all the way south to the Balkans, reaching its largest territorial extent under <a href="/wiki/Svatopluk_I_of_Moravia" title="Svatopluk I of Moravia">Svatopluk I</a> and causing a series of armed conflicts with <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">East Francia</a>. Further south, the first <a href="/wiki/South_Slavs" title="South Slavs">South Slavic states</a> emerged in the late 7th and 8th century and adopted <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>: the <a href="/wiki/First_Bulgarian_Empire" title="First Bulgarian Empire">First Bulgarian Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Serbia_(early_medieval)" title="Principality of Serbia (early medieval)">Serbian Principality</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Serbia_(medieval)" title="Kingdom of Serbia (medieval)">Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Empire" title="Serbian Empire">Empire</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Croatia" title="Duchy of Croatia">Duchy of Croatia</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Croatia_(925%E2%80%931102)" title="Kingdom of Croatia (925&#8211;1102)">Kingdom of Croatia</a>). To the East, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus" title="Kievan Rus">Kievan Rus</a> expanded from its capital in <a href="/wiki/Kiev" title="Kiev">Kiev</a> to become the largest state in Europe by the 10th century. In 988, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_the_Great" title="Vladimir the Great">Vladimir the Great</a> adopted <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christianity</a> as the religion of state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullietCrossleyHeadrickHirsch2011250_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullietCrossleyHeadrickHirsch2011250-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrownAnatoliosPalmer200966_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrownAnatoliosPalmer200966-74">[74]</a></sup> Further East, <a href="/wiki/Volga_Bulgaria" title="Volga Bulgaria">Volga Bulgaria</a> became an Islamic state in the 10th century, but was eventually absorbed into Russia several centuries later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_and_Late_Middle_Ages">High and Late Middle Ages</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Late_Middle_Ages" title="Late Middle Ages">Late Middle Ages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">Medieval demography</a></div>\n<p>The period between the year 1000 and 1300 is known as the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, during which the population of Europe experienced significant growth, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a>. Economic growth, together with the lack of safety on the mainland trading routes, made possible the development of major commercial routes along the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Seas</a>. The growing wealth and independence acquired by some coastal cities gave the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maritime_Republics" title="Maritime Republics">Maritime Republics</a> a leading role in the European scene.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philip_II_and_Tancred_meeting_in_Messina_-_British_Library_Royal_MS_16_G_vi_f350r_(detail).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="734" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Philip_II_and_Tancred_meeting_in_Messina_-_British_Library_Royal_MS_16_G_vi_f350r_%28detail%29.jpg/200px-Philip_II_and_Tancred_meeting_in_Messina_-_British_Library_Royal_MS_16_G_vi_f350r_%28detail%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Philip_II_and_Tancred_meeting_in_Messina_-_British_Library_Royal_MS_16_G_vi_f350r_%28detail%29.jpg/300px-Philip_II_and_Tancred_meeting_in_Messina_-_British_Library_Royal_MS_16_G_vi_f350r_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Philip_II_and_Tancred_meeting_in_Messina_-_British_Library_Royal_MS_16_G_vi_f350r_%28detail%29.jpg/400px-Philip_II_and_Tancred_meeting_in_Messina_-_British_Library_Royal_MS_16_G_vi_f350r_%28detail%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philip_II_and_Tancred_meeting_in_Messina_-_British_Library_Royal_MS_16_G_vi_f350r_(detail).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tancred_of_Sicily" title="Tancred of Sicily">Tancred of Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">Philip II of France</a>, during the <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third
