Major shallow water bodies submerging parts of the northern plains are the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Sea" title="Celtic Sea">Celtic Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> complex and <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>.\n</p><p>The northern plain contains the old geological continent of <a href="/wiki/Baltica" title="Baltica">Baltica</a>, and so may be regarded geologically as the "main continent", while peripheral highlands and mountainous regions in the south and west constitute fragments from various other geological continents. Most of the older geology of western Europe existed as part of the ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microcontinent" title="Microcontinent">microcontinent</a> <a href="/wiki/Avalonia" title="Avalonia">Avalonia</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span></h3>\n<p>Having lived side-by-side with agricultural peoples for millennia, Europe\'s animals and plants have been profoundly affected by the presence and activities of man. With the exception of <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">Fennoscandia</a> and northern Russia, few areas of untouched wilderness are currently found in Europe, except for various <a href="/wiki/National_park" title="National park">national parks</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_land_use_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="740" data-file-width="1004" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europe_land_use_map.png/220px-Europe_land_use_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europe_land_use_map.png/330px-Europe_land_use_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europe_land_use_map.png/440px-Europe_land_use_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_land_use_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Land use map of Europe with arable farmland (yellow), forest (dark green), pasture (light green), and tundra or bogs in the north (dark yellow)</div></div></div>\n<p>The main natural vegetation cover in Europe is mixed <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a>. The conditions for growth are very favourable. In the north, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Drift</a> warm the continent. Southern Europe could be described as having a warm, but mild climate. There are frequent summer droughts in this region. Mountain ridges also affect the conditions. Some of these (<a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>) are oriented east-west and allow the wind to carry large masses of water from the ocean in the interior. Others are oriented south-north (<a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Mountains" title="Scandinavian Mountains">Scandinavian Mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dinaric_Alps" title="Dinaric Alps">Dinarides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apennine_Mountains" title="Apennine Mountains">Apennines</a>) and because the rain falls primarily on the side of mountains that is oriented towards the sea, forests grow well on this side, while on the other side, the conditions are much less favourable. Few corners of mainland Europe have not been grazed by <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> at some point in time, and the cutting down of the pre-agricultural forest habitat caused disruption to the original plant and animal ecosystems.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Floristic_regions_in_Europe_(english).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1435" data-file-width="1797" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Floristic_regions_in_Europe_%28english%29.png/220px-Floristic_regions_in_Europe_%28english%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Floristic_regions_in_Europe_%28english%29.png/330px-Floristic_regions_in_Europe_%28english%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Floristic_regions_in_Europe_%28english%29.png/440px-Floristic_regions_in_Europe_%28english%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Floristic_regions_in_Europe_(english).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Floristic regions of Europe and neighbouring areas, according to Wolfgang Frey and Rainer L&#246;sch</div></div></div>\n<p>Probably 80 to 90 percent of Europe was once covered by forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> It stretched from the Mediterranean Sea to the Arctic Ocean. Although over half of Europe\'s original forests disappeared through the centuries of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, Europe still has over one quarter of its land area as forest, such as the <a href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest">broadleaf and mixed</a> forests, <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> of Scandinavia and Russia, mixed <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforests</a> of the Caucasus and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cork_oak" title="Cork oak">Cork oak</a> forests in the western Mediterranean. During recent times, deforestation has been slowed and many trees have been planted. However, in many cases monoculture <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">conifers</a> have replaced the original mixed natural forest, because these grow quicker. The plantations now cover vast areas of land, but offer poorer habitats for many European forest dwelling species which require a mixture of tree species and diverse forest structure. The amount of natural forest in Western Europe is just 2&#8211;3% or less, in European Russia 5&#8211;10%. The country with the smallest percentage of forested area is <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> (1%), while the most forested country is Finland (77%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In temperate Europe, mixed forest with both <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">broadleaf</a> and coniferous trees dominate. The most important species in central and western Europe are <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>. In the north, the taiga is a mixed <a href="/wiki/Spruce" title="Spruce">spruce</a>&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a>&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Birch" title="Birch">birch</a> forest; further north within Russia and extreme northern Scandinavia, the taiga gives way to <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> as the Arctic is approached. In the Mediterranean, many <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olive</a> trees have been planted, which are very well adapted to its arid climate; <a href="/wiki/Cupressus_sempervirens" title="Cupressus sempervirens">Mediterranean Cypress</a> is also widely planted in southern Europe. The semi-arid Mediterranean region hosts much scrub forest. A narrow east-west tongue of Eurasian <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a>) extends eastwards from <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and southern Russia and ends in Hungary and traverses into taiga to the north.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">Fauna</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_Europe" title="Fauna of Europe">Fauna of Europe</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_biogeography_countries.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2491" data-file-width="3535" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Europe_biogeography_countries.svg/220px-Europe_biogeography_countries.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Europe_biogeography_countries.svg/330px-Europe_biogeography_countries.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Europe_biogeography_countries.svg/440px-Europe_biogeography_countries.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_biogeography_countries.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">Biogeographic regions</a> of Europe and bordering regions</div></div></div>\n<p>Glaciation during the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">most recent ice age</a> and the presence of man affected the distribution of <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_Europe" title="Fauna of Europe">European fauna</a>. As for the animals, in many parts of Europe most large animals and top <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predator</a> species have been hunted to extinction. The <a href="/wiki/Woolly_mammoth" title="Woolly mammoth">woolly mammoth</a> was extinct before the end of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period. Today <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">wolves</a> (<a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivores</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a> (<a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>) are endangered. Once they were found in most parts of Europe. However, deforestation and hunting caused these animals to withdraw further and further. By the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> the bears\' habitats were limited to more or less inaccessible mountains with sufficient forest cover. Today, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_brown_bear" title="European brown bear">brown bear</a> lives primarily in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan" title="Balkan">Balkan peninsula</a>, Scandinavia, and Russia; a small number also persist in other countries across Europe (Austria, Pyrenees etc.), but in these areas brown bear populations are fragmented and marginalised because of the destruction of their habitat. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">polar bears</a> may be found on <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>, a Norwegian archipelago far north of Scandinavia. The <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_wolf" title="Eurasian wolf">wolf</a>, the second largest predator in Europe after the brown bear, can be found primarily in <a href="/wiki/Central_and_Eastern_Europe" title="Central and Eastern Europe">Central and Eastern Europe</a> and in the Balkans, with a handful of packs in pockets of <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> (Scandinavia, Spain, etc.).\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neandertal_-_Wisent.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Neandertal_-_Wisent.jpg/220px-Neandertal_-_Wisent.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Neandertal_-_Wisent.jpg/330px-Neandertal_-_Wisent.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Neandertal_-_Wisent.jpg/440px-Neandertal_-_Wisent.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neandertal_-_Wisent.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Once roaming the great temperate forests of Eurasia, <a href="/wiki/European_bison" title="European bison">European bison</a> now live in nature preserves in <a href="/wiki/Bia%C5%82owie%C5%BCa_Forest" title="Bia&#322;owie&#380;a Forest">Bia&#322;owie&#380;a Forest</a>, on the border between <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>European wild cat, foxes (especially the red fox), jackal and different species of martens, hedgehogs, different species of reptiles (like snakes such as vipers and grass snakes) and amphibians, different birds (owls, hawks and other birds of prey).\n</p><p>Important European herbivores are snails, larvae, fish, different birds, and mammals, like rodents, deer and roe deer, boars, and living in the mountains, marmots, steinbocks, chamois among others. A number of insects, such as the <a href="/wiki/Small_tortoiseshell" title="Small tortoiseshell">small tortoiseshell</a> butterfly, add to the biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The extinction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cretan_Dwarf_Hippopotamus" title="Cretan Dwarf Hippopotamus">dwarf hippos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_elephant" title="Dwarf elephant">dwarf elephants</a> has been linked to the earliest arrival of humans on the islands of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sea creatures are also an important part of European flora and fauna. The sea flora is mainly <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>. Important animals that live in European seas are <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Echinoderm" title="Echinoderm">echinoderms</a>, different <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octopuses" title="Octopuses">octopuses</a>, fish, <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whales" title="Whales">whales</a>.\n</p><p>Biodiversity is protected in Europe through the Council of Europe\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Conservation_of_European_Wildlife_and_Natural_Habitats" title="Convention on the Conservation of European Wildlife and Natural Habitats">Bern Convention</a>, which has also been signed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Community" title="European Community">European Community</a> as well as non-European states.\n</p><p><span id="Political_geography"></span>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Europe" title="Politics of Europe">Politics of Europe</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a Europe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_organizations_in_Europe" title="International organizations in Europe">International organizations in Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Europe" title="Regions of Europe">Regions of Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a></div>\n<div class="thumb href="/wiki/United_Nations_Interim_Administration_Mission_in_Kosovo" shape="rect" title="Kosovo (UNMIK)"/></map><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="841" data-file-width="1189" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Supranational_European_Bodies-en.svg/600px-Supranational_European_Bodies-en.svg.png 2x" usemap="#ImageMap_1_782304247" width="300"/></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supranational_European_Bodies-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A clickable <a href="/wiki/Euler_diagram" title="Euler diagram">Euler diagram</a> showing the relationships between various multinational European organisations and agreements.<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini" style="float:right"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Supranational_European_Bodies" title="Template:Supranational European Bodies"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Supranational_European_Bodies" title="Template talk:Supranational European Bodies"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Supranational_European_Bodies&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></div></div></div>\n<p>The political map of Europe is substantially derived from the re-organisation of Europe following the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> in 1815. The prevalent form of government in Europe is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a>, in most cases in the form of <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">Republic</a>; in 1815, the prevalent form of government was still the <a href="/wiki/Monarchies_in_Europe" title="Monarchies in Europe">Monarchy</a>. Europe\'s remaining eleven monarchies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> are <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/European_integration" title="European integration">European integration</a> is the process of political, legal, economic (and in some cases social and cultural) integration of European states as it has been pursued by the powers sponsoring the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> since the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>\nThe <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> has been the focus of economic integration on the continent since its foundation in 1993. More recently, the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Economic_Union" title="Eurasian Economic Union">Eurasian Economic Union</a> has been established as a counterpart comprising former Soviet states.\n</p><p>28 European states are members of the politico-economic European Union, 26 of the border-free <a href="/wiki/Schengen_Area" title="Schengen Area">Schengen Area</a> and 19 of the monetary union <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a>. Among the smaller European organizations are the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Council" title="Nordic Council">Nordic Council</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Benelux" title="Benelux">Benelux</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Assembly" title="Baltic Assembly">Baltic Assembly</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Visegr%C3%A1d_Group" title="Visegr&#225;d Group">Visegr&#225;d Group</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_states_and_territories">List of states and territories</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_in_Europe" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories in Europe">List of sovereign states and dependent territories in Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Area_and_population_of_European_countries" title="Area and population of European countries">Area and population of European countries</a></div>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/50px-Split-arrows.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/75px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/100px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that this section be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">split</a> out  into another article titled <i><a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_in_Europe" title="List of sovereign states and dependent territories in Europe">List of sovereign states and dependent territories in Europe</a></i>. style="text-align:right;">475,665\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:right;">114\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Tiraspol" title="Tiraspol">Tiraspol</a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Several dependencies and similar territories with broad autonomy are also found within or in close proximity to Europe. This includes &#197;land (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Region_of_Finland" title="Region of Finland">region of Finland</a>), two constituent countries of the Kingdom of Denmark (other than Denmark itself), three <a href="/wiki/Crown_dependencies" title="Crown dependencies">Crown dependencies</a>, and two <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territories" title="British Overseas Territories">British Overseas Territories</a>. Svalbard is also included due to its unique status within Norway, although it is not autonomous. Not included are the three <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">countries of the United Kingdom</a> with devolved powers and the two <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_Regions_of_Portugal" title="Autonomous Regions of Portugal">Autonomous Regions of Portugal</a>, which despite having a unique degree of autonomy, are not largely self-governing in matters other than international affairs. Areas with little more than a unique tax status, such as <a href="/wiki/Heligoland" title="Heligoland">Heligoland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, are also not included for this reason.\n</p>\n<table class="sortable wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th class="unsortable" style="line-height:95%; width:2em"><a href="/wiki/Flag" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe-GDP-PPP-per-capita-map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="422" data-file-width="451" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Europe-GDP-PPP-per-capita-map.png/220px-Europe-GDP-PPP-per-capita-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Europe-GDP-PPP-per-capita-map.png/330px-Europe-GDP-PPP-per-capita-map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Europe-GDP-PPP-per-capita-map.png/440px-Europe-GDP-PPP-per-capita-map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe-GDP-PPP-per-capita-map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>European and bordering nations by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">GDP</a> (PPP) per capita</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Europe" title="Economy of Europe">Economy of Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_in_Europe_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of sovereign states in Europe by GDP (nominal)">List of sovereign states in Europe by GDP (nominal)</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_in_Europe_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of sovereign states in Europe by GDP (PPP)">List of sovereign states in Europe by GDP (PPP)</a></div>\n<p>As a continent, the economy of Europe is currently the largest on Earth and it is the richest region as measured by assets under management with over $32.7&#160;trillion compared to North America\'s $27.1&#160;trillion in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> In 2009 Europe remained the wealthiest region. Its $37.1 trillion in assets under management represented one-third of the world\'s wealth. It was one of several regions where wealth surpassed its precrisis year-end peak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> As with other continents, Europe has a large variation of wealth among its countries. The richer states tend to be in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">West</a>; some of the <a href="/wiki/Central_and_Eastern_Europe" title="Central and Eastern Europe">Central and Eastern European</a> economies are still emerging from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Collapse of the Soviet Union">collapse of the Soviet Union</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia" title="Breakup of Yugoslavia">breakup of Yugoslavia</a>.\n</p><p>The European Union, a political entity composed of 28 European states, comprises the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP" title="List of countries by GDP">largest single economic area</a> in the world. 19 EU <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">countries</a> share the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> as a common currency.\nFive European countries rank in the top ten of the world\'s largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP)">national economies in GDP (PPP)</a>. This includes (ranks according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_CIA_World_Factbook" title="The CIA World Factbook">CIA</a>): Germany (5), the UK (6), Russia (7), France (8), and Italy (10).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is huge disparity between many European countries in terms of their income. The richest in terms of GDP per capita is Monaco with its US$172,676 per capita (2009) and the poorest is <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a> with its GDP per capita of US$1,631 (2010).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a> is the richest country in terms of GDP per capita in the world according to the World Bank report.\n</p><p>As a whole, Europe\'s GDP per capita is US$21,767 according to a 2016 International Monetary Fund assessment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: right; float:left; border:1px solid #aaa; margin:10px">\n<tbody><tr style="background:#dbdbdb;">\n<th>Rank\n</th>\n<th>Country\n</th>\n<th><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_IMF_ranked_countries_by_past_and_projected_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of IMF ranked countries by past and projected GDP (nominal)">GDP</a> <small>(nominal, Peak Year)</small><br/><small>millions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_dollar" title="International dollar">USD</a></small>\n</th>\n<th>Peak Year\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td></td>\n<td align="left"><i><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="810" Union</a></i></td>\n<td>19,258,227</td>\n<td>2008\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1</td>\n<td align="left"><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1000" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a></td>\n<td>551,355</td>\n<td>2018\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_history">Economic history</span></h3>\n<dl><dt>Industrial growth (1760&#8211;1945)</dt></dl>\n<p>Capitalism has been dominant in the Western world since the end of feudalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> From Britain, it gradually spread throughout Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> started in Europe, specifically the United Kingdom in the late 18th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> and the 19th century saw Western Europe industrialise. Economies were disrupted by World War I but by the beginning of World War II they had recovered and were having to compete with the growing economic strength of the United States. World War II, again, damaged much of Europe\'s industries.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Cold War (1945&#8211;1991)</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="661" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/220px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/330px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/440px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fall of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> in 1989.</div></div></div>\n<p>After World War II the economy of the UK was in a state of ruin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> and continued to suffer relative economic decline in the following decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> Italy was also in a poor economic condition but regained a high level of growth by the 1950s. West Germany <a href="/wiki/Wirtschaftswunder" title="Wirtschaftswunder">recovered quickly</a> and had doubled production from pre-war levels by the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> France also staged a remarkable comeback enjoying rapid growth and modernisation; later on Spain, under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Franco</a>, also recovered, and the nation recorded huge unprecedented economic growth beginning in the 1960s in what is called the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_miracle" title="Spanish miracle">Spanish miracle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> The majority of <a href="/wiki/Central_and_Eastern_Europe" title="Central and Eastern Europe">Central and Eastern European</a> states came under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and thus were members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_for_Mutual_Economic_Assistance" title="Council for Mutual Economic Assistance">Council for Mutual Economic Assistance</a> (COMECON).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loc-cs_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-loc-cs-207">[207]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The states which retained a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-market" title="Free-market">free-market</a> system were given a large amount of aid by the United States under the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> The western states moved to link their economies together, providing the basis for the EU and increasing cross border trade. This helped them to enjoy rapidly improving economies, while those states in COMECON were struggling in a large part due to the cost of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. Until 1990, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Community" title="European Community">European Community</a> was expanded from 6 founding members to 12. The emphasis placed on resurrecting the West German economy led to it overtaking the UK as Europe\'s largest economy.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Reunification (1991&#8211;present)</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carte_zone_euro.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="454" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Carte_zone_euro.svg/220px-Carte_zone_euro.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Carte_zone_euro.svg/330px-Carte_zone_euro.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Carte_zone_euro.svg/440px-Carte_zone_euro.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carte_zone_euro.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a> (blue colour)</div></div></div>\n<p>With the fall of communism in Central and Eastern Europe in 1991, the post-socialist states began free market reforms: Poland, Hungary, and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> adopted them reasonably quickly, while <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> and Russia are still in the process of doing so.\n</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East</a> and West Germany were reunited in 1990, the economy of West Germany struggled as it had to support and largely rebuild the infrastructure of East Germany.\n</p><p>By the millennium change, the EU dominated the economy of Europe comprising the five largest European economies of the time namely Germany, the United Kingdom, France, Italy, and Spain. In 1999, 12 of the 15 members of the EU joined the <a href="/wiki/Eurozone" title="Eurozone">Eurozone</a> replacing their former national currencies by the common euro. The three who chose to remain outside the Eurozone were: the United Kingdom, Denmark, and Sweden. The European Union is now the largest economy in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Figures released by <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> in 2009 confirmed that the Eurozone had gone into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_2000s_recession_in_Europe" title="Late 2000s recession in Europe">recession</a> in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> It impacted much of the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> In 2010, fears of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_sovereign-debt_crisis" title="European sovereign-debt crisis">sovereign debt crisis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> developed concerning some countries in Europe, especially Greece, Ireland, Spain, and Portugal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> As a result, measures were taken, especially for Greece, by the leading countries of the Eurozone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU-27</a> unemployment rate was 10.3% in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unemployment_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-unemployment-215">[215]</a></sup> For those aged 15&#8211;24 it was 22.4%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unemployment_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-unemployment-215">[215]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Europe" title="Demographics of Europe">Demographics of Europe</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_European_countries_by_population" title="List of European countries by population">List of European countries by population</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ageing_of_Europe" title="Ageing of Europe">Ageing of Europe</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Demographics_of_Europe.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="680" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Demographics_of_Europe.svg/220px-Demographics_of_Europe.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Demographics_of_Europe.svg/330px-Demographics_of_Europe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Demographics_of_Europe.svg/440px-Demographics_of_Europe.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Demographics_of_Europe.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">Population growth</a> in and around Europe in 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 2017, the population of Europe was estimated to be 742 million according to the 2017 revision of the World Population Prospects<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-1">[1]</a></sup>, which is slightly more than one-ninth of the world\'s population. This number includes Asian Russia (Siberia, about 36 million people) but excludes the European part of Turkey, Cyprus and the Transcaucasian countries.\nA century ago, Europe had nearly a quarter of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world\'s population</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Population_Growth,_1950&#8211;2050_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Population_Growth,_1950&#8211;2050-217">[217]</a></sup> The population of Europe has grown in the past century, but in other areas of the world (in particular Africa and Asia) the population has grown far more quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNPP_2006_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNPP_2006-218">[218]</a></sup> Among the continents, Europe has a relatively high <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">population density</a>, second only to Asia. Most of Europe is in a mode of <a href="/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility" title="Sub-replacement fertility">Sub-replacement fertility</a>, which means that each new(-born) generation is being less populous than the older.\nThe most densely populated country in Europe (and in the world) is the <a href="/wiki/Microstate" title="Microstate">microstate</a> of <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">Ethnic groups in Europe</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_Europe" title="Genetic history of Europe">Genetic history of Europe</a></div>\n<p>Pan and Pfeil (2004) count 87 distinct "peoples of Europe", of which 33 form the majority population in at least one sovereign state, while the remaining 54 constitute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_minority" title="Ethnic minority">ethnic minorities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>\nAccording to UN population projection, Europe\'s population may fall to about 7% of world population by 2050, or 653&#160;million people (medium variant, 556 to 777&#160;million in low and high variants, respectively).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNPP_2006_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNPP_2006-218">[218]</a></sup> Within this context, significant disparities exist between regions in relation to <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">fertility rates</a>. The average number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_fertility_rate" title="List of countries and territories by fertility rate">children per female</a> of child-bearing age is 1.52.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> According to some sources,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> this rate is higher among <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Europe" title="Islam in Europe">Muslims in Europe</a>. The UN predicts a steady <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_and_Eastern_Europe" title="Central and Eastern Europe">Central and Eastern Europe</a> as a result of emigration and low birth rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_Europe" title="Immigration to Europe">Immigration to Europe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_diaspora" title="European diaspora">European diaspora</a></div>\n<p>Europe is home to the highest number of migrants of all global regions at 70.6 million people, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Organisation_for_Migration" title="International Organisation for Migration">IOM</a>\'s report said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> In 2005, the EU had an overall net gain from <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> of 1.8&#160;million people. This accounted for almost 85% of Europe\'s total <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> The European Union plans to open the job centres for legal migrant workers from Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (April 2016)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> In 2008, 696,000 persons were given citizenship of an EU27 member state, a decrease from 707,000 the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emigration_from_Europe" title="Emigration from Europe">Emigration from Europe</a> began<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (October 2016)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Talk:Europe#Dubious" title="Talk:Europe">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> with Spanish and Portuguese settlers in the 16th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ppghis.ifcs.ufrj.br_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-ppghis.ifcs.ufrj.br-229">[229]</a></sup> and French and English settlers in the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> But numbers remained relatively small until waves of mass emigration in the 19th century, when millions of poor families left Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Today, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_diaspora" title="European diaspora">large populations of European descent</a> are found on every continent. European ancestry predominates in North America, and to a lesser degree in South America (particularly in <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, while most of the other <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin American</a> countries also have a considerable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Latin_American" title="White Latin American">population of European origins</a>). <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> have large European derived populations. Africa has no countries with European-derived majorities (or with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a> and probably <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="S&#227;o Tom&#233; and Pr&#237;ncipe">S&#227;o Tom&#233; and Pr&#237;ncipe</a>, depending on context), but there are significant minorities, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_South_African" title="White South African">White South Africans</a>. In Asia, European-derived populations predominate in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Asia" title="Northern Asia">Northern Asia</a> (specifically <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a>) and some parts of Northern <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">Languages of Europe</a></div>\n<p>Europe has about 225 indigenous languages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> mostly falling within three <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> language groups: the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>, derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>, whose ancestor language came from southern Scandinavia; and the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">Slavic languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica-186">[186]</a></sup> Slavic languages are most spoken by the number of native speakers in Europe, they are spoken in Central, Eastern, and Southeastern Europe. Romance languages are spoken primarily in south-western Europe as well as in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>, in Eastern Europe. Germanic languages are spoken in Northern Europe, the British Isles and some parts of <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica_186-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica-186">[186]</a></sup> Other Indo-European languages outside the three main groups include the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_languages" title="Baltic languages">Baltic</a> group (that is, <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a> group (that is, <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scots_Gaelic_language" title="Scots Gaelic language">Scottish Gaelic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">Breton</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica_186-3"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica-186">[186]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>.\n</p><p>A distinct non-Indo-European family of <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">Uralic languages</a> (<a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">Estonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">Finnish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erzya_language" title="Erzya language">Erzya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Komi_language" title="Komi language">Komi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mari_language" title="Mari language">Mari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moksha_language" title="Moksha language">Moksha</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Udmurt_language" title="Udmurt language">Udmurt</a>) is spoken mainly in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, and parts of Russia. <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic languages</a> include <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azerbaijani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, in addition to smaller languages in Eastern and Southeast Europe (<a href="/wiki/Balkan_Gagauz_Turkish" title="Balkan Gagauz Turkish">Balkan Gagauz Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bashkir_language" title="Bashkir language">Bashkir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chuvash_language" title="Chuvash language">Chuvash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Tatar_language" title="Crimean Tatar language">Crimean Tatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karachay-Balkar_language" title="Karachay-Balkar language">Karachay-Balkar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kumyk_language" title="Kumyk language">Kumyk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nogai_language" title="Nogai language">Nogai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tatar_language" title="Tatar language">Tatar</a>). <a href="/wiki/Kartvelian_languages" title="Kartvelian languages">Kartvelian languages</a> (<a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mingrelian_language" title="Mingrelian language">Mingrelian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Svan_language" title="Svan language">Svan</a>) are spoken primarily in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>. Two other language families reside in the North Caucasus (termed <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Caucasian_languages" title="Northeast Caucasian languages">Northeast Caucasian</a>, most notably including <a href="/wiki/Chechen_language" title="Chechen language">Chechen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avar_language" title="Avar language">Avar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lezgian_language" title="Lezgian language">Lezgin</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Caucasian_languages" title="Northwest Caucasian languages">Northwest Caucasian</a>, most notably including <a href="/wiki/Adyghe_language" title="Adyghe language">Adyghe</a>). <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">Maltese</a> is the only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semitic_language" title="Semitic language">Semitic language</a> that is official within the EU, while <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a> is the only European <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a>.\n</p><p>Multilingualism and the protection of regional and minority languages are recognised political goals in Europe today. The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> <a href="/wiki/Framework_Convention_for_the_Protection_of_National_Minorities" title="Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities">Framework Convention for the Protection of National Minorities</a> and the Council of Europe\'s <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a> set up a legal framework for language rights in Europe.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_cities">Major cities</span></h3>\n<p>The four <a href="/wiki/List_of_urban_areas_in_Europe" title="List of urban areas in Europe">most populous cities of Europe</a> are <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> and <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, each have over 10 million residents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WUP_2016_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WUP_2016-6">[6]</a></sup> and as such have been described as <a href="/wiki/Megacity" title="Megacity">megacities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> While Istanbul has the highest total population, one third lies on the Asian side of the <a href="/wiki/Bosporus" title="Bosporus">Bosporus</a>, making Moscow the most populous city entirely in Europe.\nThe next largest cities in order of population are <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, each having over 3 million residents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WUP_2016_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WUP_2016-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When considering the commuter belts or <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_in_Europe" title="List of metropolitan areas in Europe">metropolitan areas</a>, within the EU (for which comparable data is available) London covers the largest population, followed in order by Paris, Madrid, Barcelona, Berlin, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruhr_area" title="Ruhr area">Ruhr area</a>, Rome, Milan, Athens and <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_metropolitan_area" title="Warsaw metropolitan area">Warsaw</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre-Auguste_Renoir,_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1639" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg/220px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg/330px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg/440px-Pierre-Auguste_Renoir%2C_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre-Auguste_Renoir,_Le_Moulin_de_la_Galette.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Bal_du_moulin_de_la_Galette" title="Bal du moulin de la Galette">Dance at Le Moulin de la Galette</a></i>, 1876, by Pierre-Auguste Renoir</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Europe" title="Culture of Europe">Culture of Europe</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_folklore" title="European folklore">European folklore</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_art" title="European art">European art</a></div>\n<p>"Europe" as a cultural concept is substantially derived from the shared heritage of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_ancient_Rome" title="Culture of ancient Rome">its culture</a>.\nThe boundaries of Europe were historically understood as those of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> (or more specifically <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Christendom" title="Latin Christendom">Latin Christendom</a>), as established or defended throughout the medieval and early modern history of Europe, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_conquests" title="Islamic conquests">against Islam</a>, as in the <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">Reconquista</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">Ottoman wars in Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This shared cultural heritage is combined by overlapping indigenous national cultures and folklores, roughly divided into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_Europe" title="Slavic Europe">Slavic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance-speaking_Europe" title="Romance-speaking Europe">Latin (Romance)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_Europe" title="Germanic Europe">Germanic</a>, but with several components not part of either of these group (notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_culture" title="Greek culture">Greek</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celtic_Europe" title="Celtic Europe">Celtic</a>).\nCultural contact and mixtures characterise much of European regional cultures; Kaplan (2014) describes Europe as "embracing maximum cultural diversity at minimal geographical distances".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>\n<table style="float:right; clear:right; margin: 10px; border: 1px #CCCCCC solid; background:#F9F9F9">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td align="middle"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_belief_in_god.svg"><img alt="Europe belief in god.svg" data-file-height="1556" data-file-width="1700" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Europe_belief_in_god.svg/180px-Europe_belief_in_god.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Europe_belief_in_god.svg/270px-Europe_belief_in_god.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Europe_belief_in_god.svg/360px-Europe_belief_in_god.svg.png 2x" width="180"/></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Percentage of popular belief<br/>in God per European country<br/>according to the<br/>Eurobarometer (2005).\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Europe" title="Religion in Europe">Religion in Europe</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">Historically</a>, religion in Europe has been a major influence on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_art_history" title="Western art history">European art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Europe" title="Culture of Europe">culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">law</a>.\n</p><p>The largest religion in Europe is <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, with 76.2% of Europeans considering themselves <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christianity_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christianity-238">[238]</a></sup> including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a> and various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> denominations. Among Protestants, the most popular are historically state-supported European denominations such as <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reformed_faith" title="Reformed faith">Reformed faith</a>. Other Protestant denominations such as historically significant ones like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptists" title="Anabaptists">Anabaptists</a> were never supported by any state and thus are not so widespread, as well as these newly arriving from the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adventism" title="Adventism">Adventism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptists</a> and various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Protestants" title="Evangelical Protestants">Evangelical Protestants</a>; although Methodism and Baptists both have European origins. The notion of "Europe" and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_World" title="Western World">Western World</a>" has been intimately connected with the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christianity and Christendom</a>"; many even attribute Christianity for being the link that created a unified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_identity" title="European identity">European identity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica_facade,_Rome,_Italy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2975" data-file-width="4470" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica_facade%2C_Rome%2C_Italy.jpg/220px-Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica_facade%2C_Rome%2C_Italy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica_facade%2C_Rome%2C_Italy.jpg/330px-Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica_facade%2C_Rome%2C_Italy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica_facade%2C_Rome%2C_Italy.jpg/440px-Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica_facade%2C_Rome%2C_Italy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saint_Peter%27s_Basilica_facade,_Rome,_Italy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter\'s Basilica">St. Peter\'s Basilica</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a>, the largest church in the world</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, including the Roman <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> has played a prominent role in the shaping of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">Western civilization</a> since at least the 4th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caltron_J.H_Hayas_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caltron_J.H_Hayas-243">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orlandis_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orlandis-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-How_The_Catholic_Church_Built_Western_Civilization_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-How_The_Catholic_Church_Built_Western_Civilization-245">[245]</a></sup> and for at least a millennium and a half, Europe has been nearly equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Christian_culture" title="Christian culture">Christian culture</a>, even though the religion was inherited from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>. <a href="/wiki/Christian_culture" title="Christian culture">Christian culture</a> was the predominant force in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">western civilization</a>, guiding the course of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koch_1994_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koch_1994-246">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The second most popular religion is <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> (6%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> concentrated mainly in the Balkans and eastern Europe (<a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosovo" title="Kosovo">Kosovo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TRNC" title="TRNC">North Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Idel-Ural" title="Idel-Ural">Volga-Ural region</a>). Other religions, including Judaism, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> are minority religions (though Tibetan Buddhism is the majority religion of Russia\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Kalmykia" title="Republic of Kalmykia">Republic of Kalmykia</a>). The 20th century saw the revival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neopaganism" title="Neopaganism">Neopaganism</a> through movements such as <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Druidry" title="Druidry">Druidry</a>.\n</p><p>Europe has become a relatively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular" title="Secular">secular</a> continent, with an increasing number and proportion of <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">irreligious</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnostic</a> people, who make up about 18.2% of Europe\'s population,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Religiously_Unaffiliated_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-Religiously_Unaffiliated-249">[249]</a></sup> currently the largest secular population in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_religion" title="Western religion">Western world</a>. There are a particularly high number of self-described non-religious people in the Czech Republic, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, Sweden, former East Germany, and France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Europe" title="Sport in Europe">Sport in Europe</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing">Housing</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote
